<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \arctan z=z-{\frac {z^{3}}{3}}+{\frac {z^{5}}{5}}-{\frac {z^{7}}{7}}+\cdots }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \arctan z=z-{\frac {z^{3}}{3}}+{\frac {z^{5}}{5}}-{\frac {z^{7}}{7}}+\cdots }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ef0e80b93f9981701b98f8e121e27c4c50955a3d" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:35.155ex; height:5.843ex;"/></div>
</p><p>This series, sometimes called the <a href="/wiki/Leibniz_formula_for_%CF%80" title="Leibniz formula for π">Gregory–Leibniz series</a>, equals <span class="texhtml">π/4</span> when evaluated with <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i></span> = 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-LS-77">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1699, English mathematician <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Sharp" title="Abraham Sharp">Abraham Sharp</a> used the Gregory–Leibniz series for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\textstyle z={\frac {1}{\sqrt {3}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>z</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<msqrt>
<mn>3</mn>
</msqrt>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle z={\frac {1}{\sqrt {3}}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\textstyle z={\frac {1}{\sqrt {3}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4ae2125e0304d50f14a14f9559d3611557bbceaa" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:7.214ex; height:4.176ex;"/></span> to compute <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> to 71 digits, breaking the previous record of 39 digits, which was set with a polygonal algorithm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006189_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006189-78">[75]</a></sup> The Gregory–Leibniz series for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle z=1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>z</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle z=1}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="z=1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/078535cde78d90bfa1d9fbb2446204593a921d57" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.349ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is simple, but <a href="/wiki/Convergent_series" title="Convergent series">converges</a> very slowly (that is, approaches the answer gradually), so it is not used in modern <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> calculations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006156_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006156-79">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1706, <a href="/wiki/John_Machin" title="John Machin">John Machin</a> used the Gregory–Leibniz series to produce an algorithm that converged much faster:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006192–193_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006192–193-80">[77]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {\pi }{4}}=4\arctan {\frac {1}{5}}-\arctan {\frac {1}{239}}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>4</mn>
<mi>arctan</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>5</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>arctan</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>239</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {\pi }{4}}=4\arctan {\frac {1}{5}}-\arctan {\frac {1}{239}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\frac {\pi }{4}}=4\arctan {\frac {1}{5}}-\arctan {\frac {1}{239}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d1e27dbc5ad6b84571aaaae627e1d0080145e6a1" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:30.332ex; height:5.176ex;"/></div>
Machin reached 100 digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> with this formula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A72n4_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-A72n4-81">[78]</a></sup> Other mathematicians created variants, now known as <a href="/wiki/Machin-like_formula" title="Machin-like formula">Machin-like formulae</a>, that were used to set several successive records for calculating digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A72n4_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-A72n4-81">[78]</a></sup> Machin-like formulae remained the best-known method for calculating <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> well into the age of computers, and were used to set records for 250 years, culminating in a 620-digit approximation in 1946 by Daniel Ferguson – the best approximation achieved without the aid of a calculating device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006192–196,_205_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006192–196,_205-82">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1844, a record was set by <a href="/wiki/Zacharias_Dase" title="Zacharias Dase">Zacharias Dase</a>, who employed a Machin-like formula to calculate 200 decimals of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> in his head at the behest of German mathematician <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A194_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-A194-83">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1853, British mathematician <a href="/wiki/William_Shanks" title="William Shanks">William Shanks</a> calculated <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> to 607 digits, but made a mistake in the 528th digit, rendering all subsequent digits incorrect. Though he calculated an additional 100 digits in 1873, bringing the total up to 707, his previous mistake rendered all the new digits incorrect as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hayes-2014_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-hayes-2014-84">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rate_of_convergence">Rate of convergence</span></h4>
<p>Some infinite series for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> <a href="/wiki/Convergent_series" title="Convergent series">converge</a> faster than others. Given the choice of two infinite series for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, mathematicians will generally use the one that converges more rapidly because faster convergence reduces the amount of computation needed to calculate <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> to any given accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aconverge_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aconverge-85">[82]</a></sup> A simple infinite series for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Leibniz_formula_for_%CF%80" title="Leibniz formula for π">Gregory–Leibniz series</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200669–72_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200669–72-86">[83]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \pi ={\frac {4}{1}}-{\frac {4}{3}}+{\frac {4}{5}}-{\frac {4}{7}}+{\frac {4}{9}}-{\frac {4}{11}}+{\frac {4}{13}}-\cdots }" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \pi ={\frac {4}{1}}-{\frac {4}{3}}+{\frac {4}{5}}-{\frac {4}{7}}+{\frac {4}{9}}-{\frac {4}{11}}+{\frac {4}{13}}-\cdots }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \pi ={\frac {4}{1}}-{\frac {4}{3}}+{\frac {4}{5}}-{\frac {4}{7}}+{\frac {4}{9}}-{\frac {4}{11}}+{\frac {4}{13}}-\cdots }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e9e3959cd2d0ec735e7a6a1917df784842b76706" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:43.352ex; height:5.343ex;"/></div>
As individual terms of this infinite series are added to the sum, the total gradually gets closer to <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, and – with a sufficient number of terms – can get as close to <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> as desired. It converges quite slowly, though – after 500,000 terms, it produces only five correct decimal digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>An infinite series for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> (published by Nilakantha in the 15th century) that converges more rapidly than the Gregory–Leibniz series is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006Formula_16.10,_p._223_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006Formula_16.10,_p._223-88">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \pi =3+{\frac {4}{2\times 3\times 4}}-{\frac {4}{4\times 5\times 6}}+{\frac {4}{6\times 7\times 8}}-{\frac {4}{8\times 9\times 10}}+\cdots }" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>After five terms, the sum of the Gregory–Leibniz series is within 0.2 of the correct value of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, whereas the sum of Nilakantha's series is within 0.002 of the correct value. Nilakantha's series converges faster and is more useful for computing digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. Series that converge even faster include <a href="/wiki/Machin-like_formula" title="Machin-like formula">Machin's series</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chudnovsky_algorithm" title="Chudnovsky algorithm">Chudnovsky's series</a>, the latter producing 14 correct decimal digits per term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aconverge_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aconverge-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irrationality_and_transcendence">Irrationality and transcendence</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Proof_that_%CF%80_is_irrational" title="Proof that π is irrational">Proof that <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is irrational</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proof_that_%CF%80_is_transcendental" title="Proof that π is transcendental">Proof that <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is transcendental</a></div>
<p>Not all mathematical advances relating to <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> were aimed at increasing the accuracy of approximations. When Euler solved the <a href="/wiki/Basel_problem" title="Basel problem">Basel problem</a> in 1735, finding the exact value of the sum of the reciprocal squares, he established a connection between <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> and the <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a> that later contributed to the development and study of the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Posamentier_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Posamentier-90">[87]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {\pi ^{2}}{6}}={\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{4^{2}}}+\cdots }" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<msup>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mn>6</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {\pi ^{2}}{6}}={\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{4^{2}}}+\cdots }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\frac {\pi ^{2}}{6}}={\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{4^{2}}}+\cdots }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/edf829fbf86ae73080bce0a95c497001b8d15fb1" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:32.619ex; height:6.176ex;"/></div>
</p><p>Swiss scientist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Heinrich_Lambert" title="Johann Heinrich Lambert">Johann Heinrich Lambert</a> in 1768 proved that <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is <a href="/wiki/Irrational_number" title="Irrational number">irrational</a>, meaning it is not equal to the quotient of any two integers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel20065_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel20065-22">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Proof_that_%CF%80_is_irrational" title="Proof that π is irrational">Lambert's proof</a> exploited a continued-fraction representation of the tangent function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup> French mathematician <a href="/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre" title="Adrien-Marie Legendre">Adrien-Marie Legendre</a> proved in 1794 that <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span><sup>2</sup> is also irrational. In 1882, German mathematician <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_von_Lindemann" title="Ferdinand von Lindemann">Ferdinand von Lindemann</a> proved that <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_number" title="Transcendental number">transcendental</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> confirming a conjecture made by both <a href="/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre" title="Adrien-Marie Legendre">Legendre</a> and Euler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006196_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006196-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup> Hardy and Wright states that "the proofs were afterwards modified and simplified by Hilbert, Hurwitz, and other writers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Adoption_of_the_symbol_.CF.80"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Adoption_of_the_symbol_π">Adoption of the symbol <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:145px;max-width:145px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:181px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Jones,_the_Mathematician.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="632" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/William_Jones%2C_the_Mathematician.jpg/143px-William_Jones%2C_the_Mathematician.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/William_Jones%2C_the_Mathematician.jpg/215px-William_Jones%2C_the_Mathematician.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/William_Jones%2C_the_Mathematician.jpg/286px-William_Jones%2C_the_Mathematician.jpg 2x" width="143"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The earliest known use of the Greek letter π to represent the ratio of a circle's circumference to its diameter was by Welsh mathematician <a href="/wiki/William_Jones_(mathematician)" title="William Jones (mathematician)">William Jones</a> in 1706</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:143px;max-width:143px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:181px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leonhard_Euler.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="6040" data-file-width="4672" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Leonhard_Euler.jpg/141px-Leonhard_Euler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Leonhard_Euler.jpg/212px-Leonhard_Euler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Leonhard_Euler.jpg/282px-Leonhard_Euler.jpg 2x" width="141"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> popularized the use of the Greek letter π in works he published in 1736 and 1748.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In the earliest usages, the <a href="/wiki/Pi_(letter)" title="Pi (letter)">Greek letter <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></a> was used to denote the <a href="/wiki/Semiperimeter" title="Semiperimeter">semiperimeter</a> (<i>semiperipheria</i> in Latin) of a circle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firstPi_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-firstPi-9">[8]</a></sup> and was combined in ratios with <a href="/wiki/Delta_(letter)" title="Delta (letter)">δ</a> (for <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">diameter</a> or semidiameter) or <a href="/wiki/Rho" title="Rho">ρ</a> (for <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a>) to form circle constants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-96">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup> (Before then, mathematicians sometimes used letters such as <i>c</i> or <i>p</i> instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166-99">[96]</a></sup>) The first recorded use is <a href="/wiki/William_Oughtred" title="William Oughtred">Oughtred's</a> "<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \delta .\pi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>δ<!-- δ --></mi>
<mo>.</mo>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \delta .\pi }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \delta .\pi }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f01f483655135b87f3c37b5aa77d6b2e1014ce90" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.415ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span>", to express the ratio of periphery and diameter in the 1647 and later editions of <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Clavis Mathematicae</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166-99">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Barrow" title="Isaac Barrow">Barrow</a> likewise used "<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\textstyle {\frac {\pi }{\delta }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mi>δ<!-- δ --></mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle {\frac {\pi }{\delta }}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\textstyle {\frac {\pi }{\delta }}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a466800781ba6342e20a4855043f667cf417c868" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:1.778ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span>" to represent the constant 3.14...,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/David_Gregory_(mathematician)" title="David Gregory (mathematician)">Gregory</a> instead used "<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\textstyle {\frac {\pi }{\rho }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mi>ρ<!-- ρ --></mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle {\frac {\pi }{\rho }}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\textstyle {\frac {\pi }{\rho }}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f62f00c9d88b9d6b7d4401227a9289982b4820a6" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:1.778ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span>" to represent 6.28... .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-97">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest known use of the Greek letter <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> alone to represent the ratio of a circle's circumference to its diameter was by Welsh mathematician <a href="/wiki/William_Jones_(mathematician)" title="William Jones (mathematician)">William Jones</a> in his 1706 work <i><span title="Latin-language text"><span lang="la">Synopsis Palmariorum Matheseos</span></span>; or, a New Introduction to the Mathematics</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_1706_243,_263_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_1706_243,_263-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006165_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006165-103">[100]</a></sup> The Greek letter first appears there in the phrase "1/2 Periphery (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>)" in the discussion of a circle with radius one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup> However, he writes that his equations for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> are from the "ready pen of the truly ingenious Mr. <a href="/wiki/John_Machin" title="John Machin">John Machin</a>", leading to speculation that Machin may have employed the Greek letter before Jones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166-99">[96]</a></sup> Jones' notation was not immediately adopted by other mathematicians, with the fraction notation still being used as late as 1767.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-96">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euler" title="Euler">Euler</a> started using the single-letter form beginning with his 1727 <i>Essay Explaining the Properties of Air</i>, though he used <span class="nowrap"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> = 6.28...</span>, the ratio of periphery to radius, in this and some later writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup>   Euler first used <span class="nowrap"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> = 3.14...</span> in his 1736 work <i><a href="/wiki/Mechanica" title="Mechanica">Mechanica</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup> and continued in his widely-read 1748 work <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Introductio_in_analysin_infinitorum" title="Introductio in analysin infinitorum">Introductio in analysin infinitorum</a></i></span> (he wrote: "for the sake of brevity we will write this number as <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>; thus <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is equal to half the circumference of a circle of radius 1").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup> Because Euler corresponded heavily with other mathematicians in Europe, the use of the Greek letter spread rapidly, and the practice was universally adopted thereafter in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166-99">[96]</a></sup> though the definition still varied between 3.14... and 6.28... as late as 1761.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_quest_for_more_digits">Modern quest for more digits</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_era_and_iterative_algorithms">Computer era and iterative algorithms</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="; font-size: 90%;">

Then an estimate for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is given by

</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \textstyle \pi \approx {\frac {(a_{n}+b_{n})^{2}}{4t_{n}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac6a29dbd699744dac1aa6c8e615551a94c25b07" style="vertical-align: -1.505ex; width:12.808ex; height:4.843ex;"/></div>
</p>
</blockquote>
</div>
<p>The development of computers in the mid-20th century again revolutionized the hunt for digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. Mathematicians <a href="/wiki/John_Wrench" title="John Wrench">John Wrench</a> and Levi Smith reached 1,120 digits in 1949 using a desk calculator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006205_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006205-111">[108]</a></sup> Using an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverse_tangent" title="Inverse tangent">inverse tangent</a> (arctan) infinite series, a team led by George Reitwiesner and <a href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann" title="John von Neumann">John von Neumann</a> that same year achieved 2,037 digits with a calculation that took 70 hours of computer time on the <a href="/wiki/ENIAC" title="ENIAC">ENIAC</a> computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006197_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006197-112">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup> The record, always relying on an arctan series, was broken repeatedly (7,480 digits in 1957; 10,000 digits in 1958; 100,000 digits in 1961) until 1 million digits were reached in 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006197_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006197-112">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two additional developments around 1980 once again accelerated the ability to compute <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. First, the discovery of new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iterative_algorithm" title="Iterative algorithm">iterative algorithms</a> for computing <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, which were much faster than the infinite series; and second, the invention of <a href="/wiki/Multiplication_algorithm#Fast_multiplication_algorithms_for_large_inputs" title="Multiplication algorithm">fast multiplication algorithms</a> that could multiply large numbers very rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200615–17_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200615–17-114">[111]</a></sup> Such algorithms are particularly important in modern <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> computations because most of the computer's time is devoted to multiplication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006131_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006131-115">[112]</a></sup> They include the <a href="/wiki/Karatsuba_algorithm" title="Karatsuba algorithm">Karatsuba algorithm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toom%E2%80%93Cook_multiplication" title="Toom–Cook multiplication">Toom–Cook multiplication</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FFT_multiplication#Fourier_transform_methods" title="FFT multiplication">Fourier transform-based methods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006132,_140_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006132,_140-116">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>The iterative algorithms were independently published in 1975–1976 by physicist <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Salamin_(mathematician)" title="Eugene Salamin (mathematician)">Eugene Salamin</a> and scientist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_Brent_(scientist)" title="Richard Brent (scientist)">Richard Brent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200687_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200687-117">[114]</a></sup> These avoid reliance on infinite series. An iterative algorithm repeats a specific calculation, each iteration using the outputs from prior steps as its inputs, and produces a result in each step that converges to the desired value. The approach was actually invented over 160 years earlier by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a>, in what is now termed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AGM_method" title="AGM method">arithmetic–geometric mean method</a> (AGM method) or <a href="/wiki/Gauss%E2%80%93Legendre_algorithm" title="Gauss–Legendre algorithm">Gauss–Legendre algorithm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200687_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200687-117">[114]</a></sup> As modified by Salamin and Brent, it is also referred to as the Brent–Salamin algorithm.
</p><p>The iterative algorithms were widely used after 1980 because they are faster than infinite series algorithms: whereas infinite series typically increase the number of correct digits additively in successive terms, iterative algorithms generally <i>multiply</i> the number of correct digits at each step. For example, the Brent-Salamin algorithm doubles the number of digits in each iteration. In 1984, brothers <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Borwein" title="Jonathan Borwein">John</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Borwein" title="Peter Borwein">Peter Borwein</a> produced an iterative algorithm that quadruples the number of digits in each step; and in 1987, one that increases the number of digits five times in each step.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup> Iterative methods were used by Japanese mathematician <a href="/wiki/Yasumasa_Kanada" title="Yasumasa Kanada">Yasumasa Kanada</a> to set several records for computing <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> between 1995 and 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Background_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Background-119">[116]</a></sup> This rapid convergence comes at a price: the iterative algorithms require significantly more memory than infinite series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Background_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Background-119">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Motives_for_computing_.CF.80"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Motives_for_computing_π">Motives for computing <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Record_pi_approximations.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Record_pi_approximations.svg/300px-Record_pi_approximations.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Record_pi_approximations.svg/450px-Record_pi_approximations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Record_pi_approximations.svg/600px-Record_pi_approximations.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Record_pi_approximations.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>As mathematicians discovered new algorithms, and computers became available, the number of known decimal digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> increased dramatically. The vertical scale is <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">logarithmic</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>For most numerical calculations involving <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, a handful of digits provide sufficient precision. According to Jörg Arndt and Christoph Haenel, thirty-nine digits are sufficient to perform most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmological" title="Cosmological">cosmological</a> calculations, because that is the accuracy necessary to calculate the circumference of the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a> with a precision of one atom. Accounting for additional digits needed to compensate for computational <a href="/wiki/Round-off_error" title="Round-off error">round-off errors</a>, Arndt concludes that a few hundred digits would suffice for any scientific application. Despite this, people have worked strenuously to compute <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> to thousands and millions of digits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup> This effort may be partly ascribed to the human compulsion to break records, and such achievements with <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> often make headlines around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msnbc.msn.com_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc.msn.com-121">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independent.co.uk_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-independent.co.uk-122">[119]</a></sup> They also have practical benefits, such as testing <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer" title="Supercomputer">supercomputers</a>, testing numerical analysis algorithms (including <a href="/wiki/Multiplication_algorithm#Fast_multiplication_algorithms_for_large_inputs" title="Multiplication algorithm">high-precision multiplication algorithms</a>); and within pure mathematics itself, providing data for evaluating the randomness of the digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200618_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200618-123">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rapidly_convergent_series">Rapidly convergent series</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_(cleaned).jpg"><img alt="Photo portrait of a man" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_%28cleaned%29.jpg/170px-Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_%28cleaned%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_%28cleaned%29.jpg/255px-Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_%28cleaned%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_%28cleaned%29.jpg/340px-Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_%28cleaned%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_(cleaned).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Srinivasa_Ramanujan" title="Srinivasa Ramanujan">Srinivasa Ramanujan</a>, working in isolation in India, produced many innovative series for computing <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>Modern <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> calculators do not use iterative algorithms exclusively. New infinite series were discovered in the 1980s and 1990s that are as fast as iterative algorithms, yet are simpler and less memory intensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Background_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-Background-119">[116]</a></sup> The fast iterative algorithms were anticipated in 1914, when Indian mathematician <a href="/wiki/Srinivasa_Ramanujan" title="Srinivasa Ramanujan">Srinivasa Ramanujan</a> published dozens of innovative new formulae for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, remarkable for their elegance, mathematical depth and rapid convergence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup> One of his formulae, based on <a href="/wiki/Modular_equation" title="Modular equation">modular equations</a>, is

This series converges much more rapidly than most arctan series, including Machin's formula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gosper" title="Bill Gosper">Bill Gosper</a> was the first to use it for advances in the calculation of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, setting a record of 17 million digits in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup> Ramanujan's formulae anticipated the modern algorithms developed by the Borwein brothers (<a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Borwein" title="Jonathan Borwein">Jonathan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Borwein" title="Peter Borwein">Peter</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Chudnovsky_brothers" title="Chudnovsky brothers">Chudnovsky brothers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[124]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chudnovsky_algorithm" title="Chudnovsky algorithm">Chudnovsky formula</a> developed in 1987 is

It produces about 14 digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> per term,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[125]</a></sup> and has been used for several record-setting <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> calculations, including the first to surpass 1 billion (10<sup>9</sup>) digits in 1989 by the Chudnovsky brothers,  10 trillion (10<sup>13</sup>) digits in 2011 by Alexander Yee and Shigeru Kondo,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NW_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-NW-129">[126]</a></sup> and 100 trillion digits by Emma Haruka Iwao in 2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup> For similar formulas, see also the <a href="/wiki/Ramanujan%E2%80%93Sato_series" title="Ramanujan–Sato series">Ramanujan–Sato series</a>.
</p><p>In 2006, mathematician <a href="/wiki/Simon_Plouffe" title="Simon Plouffe">Simon Plouffe</a> used the PSLQ <a href="/wiki/Integer_relation_algorithm" title="Integer relation algorithm">integer relation algorithm</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[128]</a></sup> to generate several new formulas for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, conforming to the following template:

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \pi ^{k}=\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{n^{k}}}\left({\frac {a}{q^{n}-1}}+{\frac {b}{q^{2n}-1}}+{\frac {c}{q^{4n}-1}}\right),}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \pi ^{k}=\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{n^{k}}}\left({\frac {a}{q^{n}-1}}+{\frac {b}{q^{2n}-1}}+{\frac {c}{q^{4n}-1}}\right),}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/948b735a39a1bbf9a39ba0b6bd7dc63bddcd4351" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:46.161ex; height:6.843ex;"/></div>
where <span class="texhtml"><i>q</i></span> is <span class="texhtml"><a href="/wiki/Gelfond%27s_constant" title="Gelfond's constant"><i>e</i><sup><i>π</i></sup></a></span> (Gelfond's constant), <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i></span> is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odd_number" title="Odd number">odd number</a>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, <i>c</i></span> are certain rational numbers that Plouffe computed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monte_Carlo_methods">Monte Carlo methods</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:217px;max-width:217px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:133px;max-width:133px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:78px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buffon_needle.svg"><img alt="Needles of length ℓ scattered on stripes with width t" data-file-height="132" data-file-width="220" decoding="async" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Buffon_needle.svg/131px-Buffon_needle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Buffon_needle.svg/197px-Buffon_needle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Buffon_needle.svg/262px-Buffon_needle.svg.png 2x" width="131"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buffon%27s_needle" title="Buffon's needle">Buffon's needle</a>. Needles <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are dropped randomly.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:80px;max-width:80px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:78px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pi_30K.gif"><img alt="Thousands of dots randomly covering a square and a circle inscribed in the square." data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Pi_30K.gif/78px-Pi_30K.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Pi_30K.gif/117px-Pi_30K.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Pi_30K.gif/156px-Pi_30K.gif 2x" width="78"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Random dots are placed on a square and a circle inscribed inside.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monte_Carlo_methods" title="Monte Carlo methods">Monte Carlo methods</a>, which evaluate the results of multiple random trials, can be used to create approximations of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buffon%27s_needle" title="Buffon's needle">Buffon's needle</a> is one such technique: If a needle of length <span class="texhtml"><i>ℓ</i></span> is dropped <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> times on a surface on which parallel lines are drawn <span class="texhtml"><i>t</i></span> units apart, and if <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i></span> of those times it comes to rest crossing a line (<span class="texhtml"><i>x</i></span> &gt; 0), then one may approximate <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> based on the counts:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bn_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-bn-134">[131]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \pi \approx {\frac {2n\ell }{xt}}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mo>≈<!-- ≈ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>n</mi>
<mi>ℓ<!-- ℓ --></mi>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mi>x</mi>
<mi>t</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \pi \approx {\frac {2n\ell }{xt}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \pi \approx {\frac {2n\ell }{xt}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e72a1af2e84df27e3b491d5ffcf140960d0b3af" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:9.44ex; height:5.343ex;"/></div>
</p><p>Another Monte Carlo method for computing <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is to draw a circle inscribed in a square, and randomly place dots in the square. The ratio of dots inside the circle to the total number of dots will approximately equal <span class="texhtml">π/4</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Five_random_walks.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="732" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Five_random_walks.png/220px-Five_random_walks.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Five_random_walks.png/330px-Five_random_walks.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Five_random_walks.png/440px-Five_random_walks.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Five_random_walks.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Five random walks with 200 steps.  The sample mean of <span class="texhtml">|<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em;"><i>W</i><sub>200</sub></span>|</span> is <span class="texhtml">μ = 56/5</span>, and so <span class="texhtml">2(200)μ<sup>−2</sup> ≈ 3.19</span> is within <span class="texhtml">0.05</span> of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>Another way to calculate <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> using probability is to start with a <a href="/wiki/Random_walk" title="Random walk">random walk</a>, generated by a sequence of (fair) coin tosses: independent <a href="/wiki/Random_variable" title="Random variable">random variables</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>X<sub>k</sub></i></span> such that <span class="texhtml"><i>X<sub>k</sub></i> ∈ {−1,1}</span> with equal probabilities.  The associated random walk is
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle W_{n}=\sum _{k=1}^{n}X_{k}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>W</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<munderover>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>k</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</munderover>
<msub>
<mi>X</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>k</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W_{n}=\sum _{k=1}^{n}X_{k}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle W_{n}=\sum _{k=1}^{n}X_{k}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ff50676493705f91fc45cb5ab160083bbf7aa669" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:13.266ex; height:6.843ex;"/></div>
so that, for each <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">n</span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>W<sub>n</sub></i></span> is drawn from a shifted and scaled <a href="/wiki/Binomial_distribution" title="Binomial distribution">binomial distribution</a>.  As <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">n</span> varies, <span class="texhtml"><i>W<sub>n</sub></i></span> defines a (discrete) <a href="/wiki/Stochastic_process" title="Stochastic process">stochastic process</a>.  Then <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> can be calculated by<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \pi =\lim _{n\to \infty }{\frac {2n}{E[|W_{n}|]^{2}}}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \pi =\lim _{n\to \infty }{\frac {2n}{E[|W_{n}|]^{2}}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \pi =\lim _{n\to \infty }{\frac {2n}{E[|W_{n}|]^{2}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a691be63815c6b7d9fe15070ae98039d9c1d0384" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:19.402ex; height:6.009ex;"/></div>
This Monte Carlo method is independent of any relation to circles, and is a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Central_limit_theorem" title="Central limit theorem">central limit theorem</a>, discussed <a href="#Gaussian_integrals">below</a>.
</p><p>These Monte Carlo methods for approximating <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> are very slow compared to other methods, and do not provide any information on the exact number of digits that are obtained. Thus they are never used to approximate <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> when speed or accuracy is desired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spigot_algorithms">Spigot algorithms</span></h3>
<p>Two algorithms were discovered in 1995 that opened up new avenues of research into <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. They are called <a href="/wiki/Spigot_algorithm" title="Spigot algorithm">spigot algorithms</a> because, like water dripping from a <a href="/wiki/Tap_(valve)" title="Tap (valve)">spigot</a>, they produce single digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> that are not reused after they are calculated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677–84_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677–84-138">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbons_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbons-139">[136]</a></sup> This is in contrast to infinite series or iterative algorithms, which retain and use all intermediate digits until the final result is produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677–84_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677–84-138">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mathematicians <a href="/wiki/Stan_Wagon" title="Stan Wagon">Stan Wagon</a> and Stanley Rabinowitz produced a simple spigot algorithm in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbons_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbons-139">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677-140">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup> Its speed is comparable to arctan algorithms, but not as fast as iterative algorithms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677-140">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another spigot algorithm, the <a href="/wiki/Bailey%E2%80%93Borwein%E2%80%93Plouffe_formula" title="Bailey–Borwein–Plouffe formula">BBP</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digit_extraction_algorithm" title="Digit extraction algorithm">digit extraction algorithm</a>, was discovered in 1995 by Simon Plouffe:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006117,_126–128_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006117,_126–128-142">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbpf_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbpf-143">[140]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \pi =\sum _{k=0}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{16^{k}}}\left({\frac {4}{8k+1}}-{\frac {2}{8k+4}}-{\frac {1}{8k+5}}-{\frac {1}{8k+6}}\right).}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<munderover>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>k</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<msup>
<mn>16</mn>

</mrow>

</mfrac>

<mo>(</mo>
<mrow>

<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</mfrac>

<mn>6</mn>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \pi =\sum _{k=0}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{16^{k}}}\left({\frac {4}{8k+1}}-{\frac {2}{8k+4}}-{\frac {1}{8k+5}}-{\frac {1}{8k+6}}\right).}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \pi =\sum _{k=0}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{16^{k}}}\left({\frac {4}{8k+1}}-{\frac {2}{8k+4}}-{\frac {1}{8k+5}}-{\frac {1}{8k+6}}\right).}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c092c788f1ff39174a3f78c5a329d5aa83ca2add" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:54.636ex; height:7.009ex;"/></div>
This formula, unlike others before it, can produce any individual <a href="/wiki/Hexadecimal" title="Hexadecimal">hexadecimal</a> digit of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> without calculating all the preceding digits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006117,_126–128_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006117,_126–128-142">[139]</a></sup> Individual binary digits may be extracted from individual hexadecimal digits, and <a href="/wiki/Octal" title="Octal">octal</a> digits can be extracted from one or two hexadecimal digits. Variations of the algorithm have been discovered, but no digit extraction algorithm has yet been found that rapidly produces decimal digits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup> An important application of digit extraction algorithms is to validate new claims of record <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> computations: After a new record is claimed, the decimal result is converted to hexadecimal, and then a digit extraction algorithm is used to calculate several random hexadecimal digits near the end; if they match, this provides a measure of confidence that the entire computation is correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NW_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-NW-129">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1998 and 2000, the <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> project <a href="/wiki/PiHex" title="PiHex">PiHex</a> used <a href="/wiki/Bellard%27s_formula" title="Bellard's formula">Bellard's formula</a> (a modification of the BBP algorithm) to compute the quadrillionth (10<sup>15</sup>th) bit of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, which turned out to be 0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[142]</a></sup> In September 2010, a <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a> employee used the company's <a href="/wiki/Apache_Hadoop" title="Apache Hadoop">Hadoop</a> application on one thousand computers over a 23-day period to compute 256 <a href="/wiki/Bit" title="Bit">bits</a> of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> at the two-quadrillionth (2×10<sup>15</sup>th) bit, which also happens to be zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_and_characterizations_in_mathematics">Role and characterizations in mathematics</span></h2>
<p>Because <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is closely related to the circle, it is found in <a href="/wiki/List_of_formulae_involving_%CF%80" title="List of formulae involving π">many formulae</a> from the fields of geometry and trigonometry, particularly those concerning circles, spheres, or ellipses. Other branches of science, such as statistics, physics, <a href="/wiki/Fourier_analysis" title="Fourier analysis">Fourier analysis</a>, and number theory, also include <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> in some of their important formulae.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry_and_trigonometry">Geometry and trigonometry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Circle_Area.svg"><img alt="A diagram of a circle with a square coving the circle's upper right quadrant." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="264" data-file-width="264" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Circle_Area.svg/220px-Circle_Area.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Circle_Area.svg/330px-Circle_Area.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Circle_Area.svg/440px-Circle_Area.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Circle_Area.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The area of the circle equals <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> times the shaded area. The area of the <a href="/wiki/Unit_circle" title="Unit circle">unit circle</a> is <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.</div></div></div>
<p><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> appears in formulae for areas and volumes of geometrical shapes based on circles, such as <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">spheres</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cone_(geometry)" title="Cone (geometry)">cones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Torus" title="Torus">tori</a>. Below are some of the more common formulae that involve <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The circumference of a circle with radius <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> is <span class="texhtml">2π<i>r</i></span>.</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Area_of_a_disk" title="Area of a disk">area of a circle</a> with radius <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> is <span class="texhtml">π<i>r</i><sup>2</sup></span>.</li>
<li>The area of an ellipse with semi-major axis <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i></span> and semi-minor axis <span class="texhtml"><i>b</i></span> is <span class="texhtml">π<i>ab</i></span>.</li>
<li>The volume of a sphere with radius <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> is <span class="texhtml"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">4</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span>π<i>r</i><sup>3</sup></span>.</li>
<li>The surface area of a sphere with radius <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> is <span class="texhtml">4π<i>r</i><sup>2</sup></span>.</li></ul>
<p>Some of the formulae above are special cases of the volume of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-ball" title="N-ball"><i>n</i>-dimensional ball</a> and the surface area of its boundary, the <a href="/wiki/N-sphere" title="N-sphere">(<i>n</i>−1)-dimensional sphere</a>, given <a href="#The_gamma_function_and_Stirling's_approximation">below</a>.
</p><p>Apart from circles, there are other <a href="/wiki/Curve_of_constant_width" title="Curve of constant width">curves of constant width</a>. By <a href="/wiki/Barbier%27s_theorem" title="Barbier's theorem">Barbier's theorem</a>, every curve of constant width has perimeter <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> times its width.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartiniMontejanoOliveros2019Corollary_5.1.1,_p._98_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartiniMontejanoOliveros2019Corollary_5.1.1,_p._98-148">[145]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Reuleaux_triangle" title="Reuleaux triangle">Reuleaux triangle</a> (formed by the intersection of three circles with the sides of an <a href="/wiki/Equilateral_triangle" title="Equilateral triangle">equilateral triangle</a> as their radii)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartiniMontejanoOliveros20193_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartiniMontejanoOliveros20193-149">[146]</a></sup> has the smallest possible area for its width and the circle the largest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartiniMontejanoOliveros201910_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartiniMontejanoOliveros201910-150">[147]</a></sup> There also exist non-circular <a href="/wiki/Smoothness" title="Smoothness">smooth</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_curve" title="Algebraic curve">algebraic curves</a> of constant width.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartiniMontejanoOliveros2019Section_5.3.3:_The_analytic_curve_of_constant_width_due_to_Rabinowitz,_pp._111–112_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartiniMontejanoOliveros2019Section_5.3.3:_The_analytic_curve_of_constant_width_due_to_Rabinowitz,_pp._111–112-151">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">Definite integrals</a> that describe circumference, area, or volume of shapes generated by circles typically have values that involve <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. For example, an integral that specifies half the area of a circle of radius one is given by:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup>

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Units_of_angle">Units of angle</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Units_of_angle_measure" title="Units of angle measure">Units of angle measure</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sine_cosine_one_period.svg"><img alt="Diagram showing graphs of functions" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sine_cosine_one_period.svg/340px-Sine_cosine_one_period.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sine_cosine_one_period.svg/510px-Sine_cosine_one_period.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sine_cosine_one_period.svg/680px-Sine_cosine_one_period.svg.png 2x" width="340"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sine_cosine_one_period.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">Sine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosine" title="Cosine">cosine</a> functions repeat with period 2<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.</div></div></div><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigonometric_function" title="Trigonometric function">trigonometric functions</a> rely on angles, and mathematicians generally use radians as units of measurement. <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> plays an important role in angles measured in <a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">radians</a>, which are defined so that a complete circle spans an angle of 2<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> radians. The angle measure of 180° is equal to <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> radians, and 1° = <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>/180 radians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbramson2014[httpsopenstaxorgbooksprecalculuspages5-1-angles_Section_5.1:_Angles]_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbramson2014[httpsopenstaxorgbooksprecalculuspages5-1-angles_Section_5.1:_Angles]-153">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Common trigonometric functions have periods that are multiples of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>; for example, sine and cosine have period 2<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCS_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-WCS-154">[151]</a></sup> so for any angle <span class="texhtml"><i>θ</i></span> and any integer <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i></span>,
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \sin \theta =\sin \left(\theta +2\pi k\right){\text{ and }}\cos \theta =\cos \left(\theta +2\pi k\right).}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eigenvalues">Eigenvalues</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg/220px-Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg/330px-Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg/440px-Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Overtone" title="Overtone">overtones</a> of a vibrating string are <a href="/wiki/Eigenfunction" title="Eigenfunction">eigenfunctions</a> of the second derivative, and form a <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_series_(music)" title="Harmonic series (music)">harmonic progression</a>.  The associated eigenvalues form the <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_progression" title="Arithmetic progression">arithmetic progression</a> of integer multiples of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>Many of the appearances of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> in the formulas of mathematics and the sciences have to do with its close relationship with geometry.  However, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> also appears in many natural situations having apparently nothing to do with geometry.
</p><p>In many applications, it plays a distinguished role as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eigenvalue" title="Eigenvalue">eigenvalue</a>.  For example, an idealized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrating_string" title="Vibrating string">vibrating string</a> can be modelled as the graph of a function <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i></span> on the unit interval <span class="texhtml">[0, 1]</span>, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boundary_conditions" title="Boundary conditions">fixed ends</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(0) = <i>f</i>(1) = 0</span>. The modes of vibration of the string are solutions of the <a href="/wiki/Differential_equation" title="Differential equation">differential equation</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f''(x)+\lambda f(x)=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</p><p>The value <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is, in fact, the <i>least</i> such value of the wavenumber, and is associated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_mode" title="Fundamental mode">fundamental mode</a> of vibration of the string.  One way to show this is by estimating the <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, which satisfies <a href="/wiki/Wirtinger%27s_inequality_for_functions" title="Wirtinger's inequality for functions">Wirtinger's inequality</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDymMcKean197247_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDymMcKean197247-156">[153]</a></sup> for a function <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f:[0,1]\to \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \pi ^{2}\int _{0}^{1}|f(x)|^{2}\,dx\leq \int _{0}^{1}|f'(x)|^{2}\,dx,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9021233d9bdca65d39dc801347f5901f04e55204" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:34.455ex; height:6.176ex;"/></div>
</p><p>with equality precisely when <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i></span> is a multiple of <span class="texhtml">sin(π <i>x</i>)</span>. Here <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> appears as an optimal constant in Wirtinger's inequality, and it follows that it is the smallest wavenumber, using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Variational_theorem" title="Variational theorem">variational characterization</a> of the eigenvalue. As a consequence, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is the smallest <a href="/wiki/Singular_value" title="Singular value">singular value</a> of the derivative operator on the space of functions on <span class="texhtml">[0, 1]</span> vanishing at both endpoints (the <a href="/wiki/Sobolev_space" title="Sobolev space">Sobolev space</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle H_{0}^{1}[0,1]}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msubsup>
<mi>H</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msubsup>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H_{0}^{1}[0,1]}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle H_{0}^{1}[0,1]}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7113d194de39d54621e9da47782ad5263b9f1790" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:7.81ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>).
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inequalities">Inequalities</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sir_William_Thompson_illustration_of_Carthage.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="287" data-file-width="286" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Sir_William_Thompson_illustration_of_Carthage.png/220px-Sir_William_Thompson_illustration_of_Carthage.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d8/Sir_William_Thompson_illustration_of_Carthage.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sir_William_Thompson_illustration_of_Carthage.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">ancient city of Carthage</a> was the solution to an isoperimetric problem, according to a legend recounted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Kelvin" title="Lord Kelvin">Lord Kelvin</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup> those lands bordering the sea that <a href="/wiki/Dido" title="Dido">Queen Dido</a> could enclose on all other sides within a single given oxhide, cut into strips.</div></div></div>
<p>The number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> serves appears in similar eigenvalue problems in higher-dimensional analysis.  As mentioned <a href="#Definition">above</a>, it can be characterized via its role as the best constant in the <a href="/wiki/Isoperimetric_inequality" title="Isoperimetric inequality">isoperimetric inequality</a>: the area <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">A</span> enclosed by a plane <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jordan_curve" title="Jordan curve">Jordan curve</a> of perimeter <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">P</span> satisfies the inequality
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 4\pi A\leq P^{2},}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>4</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mi>A</mi>
<mo>≤<!-- ≤ --></mo>
<msup>
<mi>P</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 4\pi A\leq P^{2},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle 4\pi A\leq P^{2},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2da151eb3d06c6538dfa721d85c36e2b052a5114" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:10.859ex; height:3.009ex;"/></div>
and equality is clearly achieved for the circle, since in that case <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i> = π<i>r</i><sup>2</sup></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>P</i> = 2π<i>r</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ultimately as a consequence of the isoperimetric inequality, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> appears in the optimal constant for the critical <a href="/wiki/Sobolev_inequality" title="Sobolev inequality">Sobolev inequality</a> in <i>n</i> dimensions, which thus characterizes the role of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> in many physical phenomena as well, for example those of classical <a href="/wiki/Potential_theory" title="Potential theory">potential theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[158]</a></sup>  In two dimensions, the critical Sobolev inequality is
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 2\pi \|f\|_{2}\leq \|\nabla f\|_{1}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

for <i>f</i> a smooth function with compact support in <span class="texhtml"><b>R</b><sup>2</sup></span>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \nabla f}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

The Sobolev inequality is equivalent to the isoperimetric inequality (in any dimension), with the same best constants.
</p><p>Wirtinger's inequality also generalizes to higher-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9_inequality" title="Poincaré inequality">Poincaré inequalities</a> that provide best constants for the <a href="/wiki/Dirichlet_energy" title="Dirichlet energy">Dirichlet energy</a> of an <i>n</i>-dimensional membrane.
