for all <a href="/wiki/Convex_set" title="Convex set">convex</a> subsets <span class="texhtml"><i>G</i></span> of <span class="texhtml"><b>R</b><sup><i>n</i></sup></span> of diameter 1, and square-integrable functions <i>u</i> on <span class="texhtml"><i>G</i></span> of mean zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup>  Just as Wirtinger's inequality is the <a href="/wiki/Calculus_of_variations" title="Calculus of variations">variational</a> form of the <a href="/wiki/Dirichlet_eigenvalue" title="Dirichlet eigenvalue">Dirichlet eigenvalue</a> problem in one dimension, the Poincaré inequality is the variational form of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neumann_problem" title="Neumann problem">Neumann</a> eigenvalue problem, in any dimension.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourier_transform_and_Heisenberg_uncertainty_principle">Fourier transform and Heisenberg uncertainty principle</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Heisenberg_geodesic.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="315" decoding="async" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Animation_of_Heisenberg_geodesic.gif/220px-Animation_of_Heisenberg_geodesic.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/Animation_of_Heisenberg_geodesic.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Heisenberg_geodesic.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An animation of a <a href="/wiki/Heisenberg_group#As_a_sub-Riemannian_manifold" title="Heisenberg group">geodesic in the Heisenberg group</a></div></div></div>
<p>The constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> also appears as a critical spectral parameter in the <a href="/wiki/Fourier_transform" title="Fourier transform">Fourier transform</a>.  This is the <a href="/wiki/Integral_transform" title="Integral transform">integral transform</a>, that takes a complex-valued integrable function <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i></span> on the real line to the function defined as:

</p><p>Although there are several different conventions for the Fourier transform and its inverse, any such convention must involve <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> <i>somewhere</i>. The above is the most canonical definition, however, giving the unique unitary operator on <span class="texhtml"><i>L</i><sup>2</sup></span> that is also an algebra homomorphism of <span class="texhtml"><i>L</i><sup>1</sup></span> to <span class="texhtml"><i>L</i><sup>∞</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heisenberg_uncertainty_principle" title="Heisenberg uncertainty principle">Heisenberg uncertainty principle</a> also contains the number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. </semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \left(\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }x^{2}|f(x)|^{2}\,dx\right)\left(\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }\xi ^{2}|{\hat {f}}(\xi )|^{2}\,d\xi \right)\geq \left({\frac {1}{4\pi }}\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }|f(x)|^{2}\,dx\right)^{2}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/08ed97af513c67ad7eb0f44a51c76696255f16ab" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:67.056ex; height:6.509ex;"/></div>
The physical consequence, about the uncertainty in simultaneous position and momentum observations of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanical" title="Quantum mechanical">quantum mechanical</a> system, is <a href="#Describing_physical_phenomena">discussed below</a>.  The appearance of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> in the formulae of Fourier analysis is ultimately a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Stone%E2%80%93von_Neumann_theorem" title="Stone–von Neumann theorem">Stone–von Neumann theorem</a>, asserting the uniqueness of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_representation" title="Schrödinger representation">Schrödinger representation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Heisenberg_group" title="Heisenberg group">Heisenberg group</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howe_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-howe-164">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaussian_integrals">Gaussian integrals</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E%5E(-x%5E2).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg/220px-E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg/330px-E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg/440px-E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E%5E(-x%5E2).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A graph of the <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_function" title="Gaussian function">Gaussian function</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>ƒ</i>(<i>x</i>) = <i>e</i><sup>−<i>x</i><sup>2</sup></sup></span>. The coloured region between the function and the <i>x</i>-axis has area <span class="texhtml"><span class="nowrap">√<span style="border-top:1px solid; padding:0 0.1em;">π</span></span></span>.</div></div></div>
<p>The fields of <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a> and <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> frequently use the <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normal distribution</a> as a simple model for complex phenomena; for example, scientists generally assume that the observational error in most experiments follows a normal distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[162]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_function" title="Gaussian function">Gaussian function</a>, which is the <a href="/wiki/Probability_density_function" title="Probability density function">probability density function</a> of the normal distribution with <a href="/wiki/Mean" title="Mean">mean</a> <span class="texhtml">μ</span> and <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviation</a> <span class="texhtml">σ</span>, naturally contains <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GaussProb_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-GaussProb-166">[163]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f(x)={1 \over \sigma {\sqrt {2\pi }}}\,e^{-(x-\mu )^{2}/(2\sigma ^{2})}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</p><p>The factor of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{\sqrt {2\pi }}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

This follows from a <a href="/wiki/Integration_by_substitution" title="Integration by substitution">change of variables</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_integral" title="Gaussian integral">Gaussian integral</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GaussProb_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-GaussProb-166">[163]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \int _{-\infty }^{\infty }e^{-u^{2}}\,du={\sqrt {\pi }}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</msqrt>
</mrow>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \int _{-\infty }^{\infty }e^{-u^{2}}\,du={\sqrt {\pi }}}</annotation>

</p><p>which says that the area under the basic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bell_curve" title="Bell curve">bell curve</a> in the figure is equal to the square root of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Central_limit_theorem" title="Central limit theorem">central limit theorem</a> explains the central role of normal distributions, and thus of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, in probability and statistics. This theorem is ultimately connected with the <a href="#Fourier_transform_and_Heisenberg_uncertainty_principle">spectral characterization</a> of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> as the eigenvalue associated with the Heisenberg uncertainty principle, and the fact that equality holds in the uncertainty principle only for the Gaussian function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDymMcKean1972Section_2.7_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDymMcKean1972Section_2.7-167">[164]</a></sup> Equivalently, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is the unique constant making the Gaussian normal distribution <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i><sup>-π<i>x</i><sup>2</sup></sup></span> equal to its own Fourier transform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[165]</a></sup> Indeed,  according to <a href="#CITEREFHowe1980">Howe (1980)</a>, the "whole business" of establishing the fundamental theorems of Fourier analysis reduces to the Gaussian integral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howe_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-howe-164">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Projective_geometry">Projective geometry</span></h3>
<p>Let <span class="texhtml"><i>V</i></span> be the set of all twice differentiable real functions <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f:\mathbb {R} \to \mathbb {R} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

This function is periodic, and the quantity <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> can be characterized as the period of this map.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topology">Topology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg/220px-Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg/330px-Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg/440px-Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Uniformization_theorem" title="Uniformization theorem">Uniformization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Klein_quartic" title="Klein quartic">Klein quartic</a>, a surface of <a href="/wiki/Genus_(mathematics)" title="Genus (mathematics)">genus</a> three and Euler characteristic −4, as a quotient of the <a href="/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9_disk_model" title="Poincaré disk model">hyperbolic plane</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_group" title="Symmetry group">symmetry group</a> <a href="/wiki/PSL(2,7)" title="PSL(2,7)">PSL(2,7)</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Fano_plane" title="Fano plane">Fano plane</a>.  The hyperbolic area of a fundamental domain is <span class="texhtml">8π</span>, by Gauss–Bonnet.</div></div></div>
<p>The constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> appears in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gauss%E2%80%93Bonnet_formula" title="Gauss–Bonnet formula">Gauss–Bonnet formula</a> which relates the <a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry_of_surfaces" title="Differential geometry of surfaces">differential geometry of surfaces</a> to their <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a>.  Specifically, if a <a href="/wiki/Compact_space" title="Compact space">compact</a> surface <span class="texhtml">Σ</span> has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gauss_curvature" title="Gauss curvature">Gauss curvature</a> <i>K</i>, then
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \int _{\Sigma }K\,dA=2\pi \chi (\Sigma )}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>

</mrow>

<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \int _{\Sigma }K\,dA=2\pi \chi (\Sigma )}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \int _{\Sigma }K\,dA=2\pi \chi (\Sigma )}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/00c846ab9590f095656ecf41398c7d91b9f3a00e" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:19.046ex; height:5.676ex;"/></div>
where <span class="texhtml"><i>χ</i>(Σ)</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Euler_characteristic" title="Euler characteristic">Euler characteristic</a>, which is an integer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[167]</a></sup>  An example is the surface area of a sphere <i>S</i> of curvature 1 (so that its <a href="/wiki/Radius_of_curvature" title="Radius of curvature">radius of curvature</a>, which coincides with its radius, is also 1.)  The Euler characteristic of a sphere can be computed from its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homology_group" title="Homology group">homology groups</a> and is found to be equal to two.  Thus we have
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle A(S)=\int _{S}1\,dA=2\pi \cdot 2=4\pi }" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A(S)=\int _{S}1\,dA=2\pi \cdot 2=4\pi }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle A(S)=\int _{S}1\,dA=2\pi \cdot 2=4\pi }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f541185019255fc11e6ef909d681e99b9490cf88" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:29.658ex; height:5.676ex;"/></div>
reproducing the formula for the surface area of a sphere of radius 1.
</p><p>The constant appears in many other integral formulae in topology, in particular, those involving <a href="/wiki/Characteristic_class" title="Characteristic class">characteristic classes</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Chern%E2%80%93Weil_homomorphism" title="Chern–Weil homomorphism">Chern–Weil homomorphism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vector_calculus">Vector calculus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YL10M5sph.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/YL10M5sph.png/220px-YL10M5sph.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/YL10M5sph.png/330px-YL10M5sph.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/YL10M5sph.png/440px-YL10M5sph.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:YL10M5sph.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Decompositions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spherical_harmonic" title="Spherical harmonic">spherical harmonics</a>, an area in vector calculus</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vector_calculus" title="Vector calculus">Vector calculus</a> is a branch of calculus that is concerned with the properties of <a href="/wiki/Vector_field" title="Vector field">vector fields</a>, and has many physical applications such as to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electricity_and_magnetism" title="Electricity and magnetism">electricity and magnetism</a>.  The <a href="/wiki/Newtonian_potential" title="Newtonian potential">Newtonian potential</a> for a point source <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">Q</span> situated at the origin of a three-dimensional Cartesian coordinate system is<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[169]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle V(\mathbf {x} )=-{\frac {kQ}{|\mathbf {x} |}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle V(\mathbf {x} )=-{\frac {kQ}{|\mathbf {x} |}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle V(\mathbf {x} )=-{\frac {kQ}{|\mathbf {x} |}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c6604a00bc259c544995c0a7c77456c5adb65498" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:13.8ex; height:6.176ex;"/></div>
which represents the <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> of a unit mass (or charge) placed a distance <span class="texhtml">|<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em;"><b>x</b></span>|</span> from the source, and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">k</span> is a dimensional constant.  The field, denoted here by <span class="texhtml"><b>E</b></span>, which may be the (Newtonian) <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">gravitational field</a> or the (Coulomb) <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a>, is the negative <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">gradient</a> of the potential:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {E} =-\nabla V.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">E</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∇<!-- ∇ --></mi>
<mi>V</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {E} =-\nabla V.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbf {E} =-\nabla V.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ada9f322050f63ee61d24edf0ed0337a9f3565a7" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:11.034ex; height:2.343ex;"/></div>
Special cases include <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb's law">Coulomb's law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton's law of universal gravitation">Newton's law of universal gravitation</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gauss%27_law" title="Gauss' law">Gauss' law</a> states that the outward <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">flux</a> of the field through any smooth, simple, closed, orientable surface <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">S</span> containing the origin is equal to <span class="texhtml">4<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span><i>kQ</i></span>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="nowrap"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 4\pi kQ=}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbf {E} \cdot d\mathbf {A} .}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3e6f321b8b2ed319cd5cd13bb1dd3e60d784dc32" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.318ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>It is standard to absorb this factor of <span class="texhtml">4π</span> into the constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">k</span>, but this argument shows why it must appear <i>somewhere</i>.  Furthermore, <span class="texhtml">4π</span> is the surface area of the unit sphere, but we have not assumed that <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">S</span> is the sphere.  However, as a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Divergence_theorem" title="Divergence theorem">divergence theorem</a>, because the region away from the origin is vacuum (source-free) it is only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homology_class" title="Homology class">homology class</a> of the surface <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">S</span> in <span class="texhtml"><b>R</b><sup>3</sup>\{0} </span> that matters in computing the integral, so it can be replaced by any convenient surface in the same homology class, in particular, a sphere, where spherical coordinates can be used to calculate the integral.
</p><p>A consequence of the Gauss law is that the negative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laplacian" title="Laplacian">Laplacian</a> of the potential <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">V</span> is equal to <span class="texhtml">4π<i>kQ</i></span> times the <a href="/wiki/Dirac_delta_function" title="Dirac delta function">Dirac delta function</a>:

More general distributions of matter (or charge) are obtained from this by <a href="/wiki/Convolution" title="Convolution">convolution</a>, giving the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poisson_equation" title="Poisson equation">Poisson equation</a>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta V(\mathbf {x} )=-4\pi k\rho (\mathbf {x} )}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Delta V(\mathbf {x} )=-4\pi k\rho (\mathbf {x} )}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \Delta V(\mathbf {x} )=-4\pi k\rho (\mathbf {x} )}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2d5fdc6a320a8af1912a28773730e14f8cf2b7b3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:19.978ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>
where <span class="texhtml">ρ</span> is the distribution function.
</p><p>The constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> also plays an analogous role in four-dimensional potentials associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein%27s_equations" title="Einstein's equations">Einstein's equations</a>, a fundamental formula which forms the basis of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_theory_of_relativity" title="General theory of relativity">general theory of relativity</a> and describes the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental interaction</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> as a result of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> being <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">curved</a> by <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[171]</a></sup>

</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle R_{\mu \nu }-{\frac {1}{2}}Rg_{\mu \nu }+\Lambda g_{\mu \nu }={\frac {8\pi G}{c^{4}}}T_{\mu \nu },}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/504a7090e837638f5c316aa2a3c1913013f06ff4" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:33.677ex; height:5.676ex;"/></div>
where <span class="texhtml"><i>R</i><sub><i>μν</i></sub></span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ricci_curvature_tensor" title="Ricci curvature tensor">Ricci curvature tensor</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">R</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Scalar_curvature" title="Scalar curvature">scalar curvature</a>, <span class="texhtml"><i>g</i><sub><i>μν</i></sub></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Metric_tensor_(general_relativity)" title="Metric tensor (general relativity)">metric tensor</a>, <span class="texhtml">Λ</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">G</span> is <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">Newton's gravitational constant</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">c</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> in vacuum, and <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i><sub><i>μν</i></sub></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Stress%E2%80%93energy_tensor" title="Stress–energy tensor">stress–energy tensor</a>.  The left-hand side of Einstein's equation is a non-linear analogue of the Laplacian of the metric tensor, and reduces to that in the weak field limit, with the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Lambda g}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">Λ<!-- Λ --></mi>
<mi>g</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Lambda g}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \Lambda g}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0dcdc50679aaff78e6bf0ae7c45d58af26cf5c2a" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.729ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> term playing the role of a <a href="/wiki/Lagrange_multiplier" title="Lagrange multiplier">Lagrange multiplier</a>, and the right-hand side is the analogue of the distribution function, times <span class="texhtml">8π</span>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Cauchy.27s_integral_formula"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cauchy's_integral_formula">Cauchy's integral formula</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Factorial05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="598" data-file-width="760" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Factorial05.jpg/220px-Factorial05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Factorial05.jpg/330px-Factorial05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Factorial05.jpg/440px-Factorial05.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Factorial05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Complex analytic functions can be visualized as a collection of streamlines and equipotentials, systems of curves intersecting at right angles.  Here illustrated is the complex logarithm of the Gamma function.</div></div></div>
<p>One of the key tools in <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex analysis</a> is <a href="/wiki/Contour_integration" title="Contour integration">contour integration</a> of a function over a positively oriented (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rectifiable_curve" title="Rectifiable curve">rectifiable</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jordan_curve" title="Jordan curve">Jordan curve</a> <span class="texhtml">γ</span>.  A form of <a href="/wiki/Cauchy%27s_integral_formula" title="Cauchy's integral formula">Cauchy's integral formula</a> states that if a point <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i><sub>0</sub></span> is interior to <span class="texhtml">γ</span>, then<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[172]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \oint _{\gamma }{\frac {dz}{z-z_{0}}}=2\pi i.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mo>∮<!-- ∮ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>γ<!-- γ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>

<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msub>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mi>i</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \oint _{\gamma }{\frac {dz}{z-z_{0}}}=2\pi i.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \oint _{\gamma }{\frac {dz}{z-z_{0}}}=2\pi i.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/17569b627f1050cac4952724e6e56209e44dc425" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:16.747ex; height:6.176ex;"/></div>
</p><p>Although the curve <span class="texhtml">γ</span> is not a circle, and hence does not have any obvious connection to the constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, a standard proof of this result uses <a href="/wiki/Morera%27s_theorem" title="Morera's theorem">Morera's theorem</a>, which implies that the integral is invariant under <a href="/wiki/Homotopy" title="Homotopy">homotopy</a> of the curve, so that it can be deformed to a circle and then integrated explicitly in polar coordinates.  More generally, it is true that if a rectifiable closed curve <span class="texhtml">γ</span> does not contain <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i><sub>0</sub></span>, then the above integral is <span class="texhtml">2π<i>i</i></span> times the <a href="/wiki/Winding_number" title="Winding number">winding number</a> of the curve.
</p><p>The general form of Cauchy's integral formula establishes the relationship between the values of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_analytic_function" title="Complex analytic function">complex analytic function</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>z</i>)</span> on the Jordan curve <span class="texhtml">γ</span> and the value of <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>z</i>)</span> at any interior point <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i><sub>0</sub></span> of <span class="texhtml">γ</span>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[173]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \oint _{\gamma }{f(z) \over z-z_{0}}\,dz=2\pi if(z_{0})}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

provided <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>z</i>)</span> is analytic in the region enclosed by <span class="texhtml">γ</span> and extends continuously to <span class="texhtml">γ</span>.  Cauchy's integral formula is a special case of the <a href="/wiki/Residue_theorem" title="Residue theorem">residue theorem</a>, that if <span class="texhtml"><i>g</i>(<i>z</i>)</span> is a <a href="/wiki/Meromorphic_function" title="Meromorphic function">meromorphic function</a> the region enclosed by <span class="texhtml">γ</span> and is continuous in a neighbourhood of <span class="texhtml">γ</span>, then
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \oint _{\gamma }g(z)\,dz=2\pi i\sum \operatorname {Res} (g,a_{k})}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h3><span id="The_gamma_function_and_Stirling.27s_approximation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_gamma_function_and_Stirling's_approximation">The gamma function and Stirling's approximation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg/220px-Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg/330px-Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg/440px-Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plot of the gamma function on the real axis</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Factorial" title="Factorial">factorial</a> function <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle n!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>!</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n!}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="n!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bae971720be3cc9b8d82f4cdac89cb89877514a6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.042ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the product of all of the positive integers through <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span>. The <a href="/wiki/Gamma_function" title="Gamma function">gamma function</a> extends the concept of <a href="/wiki/Factorial" title="Factorial">factorial</a> (normally defined only for non-negative integers) to all complex numbers, except the negative real integers, with the identity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Gamma (n)=(n-1)!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mi>n</mi>

</semantics>

When the gamma function is evaluated at half-integers, the result contains <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. For example, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Gamma (1/2)={\sqrt {\pi }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>The gamma function is defined by its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weierstrass_product" title="Weierstrass product">Weierstrass product</a> development:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[175]</a></sup>

where <span class="texhtml">γ</span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euler%E2%80%93Mascheroni_constant" title="Euler–Mascheroni constant">Euler–Mascheroni constant</a>.  Evaluated at <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = 1/2</span> and squared, the equation <span class="texhtml">Γ(1/2)<sup>2</sup> = π</span> reduces to the Wallis product formula.  The gamma function is also connected to the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a> and identities for the <a href="/wiki/Functional_determinant" title="Functional determinant">functional determinant</a>, in which the constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> <a href="#Number_theory_and_Riemann_zeta_function">plays an important role</a>.
</p><p>The gamma function is used to calculate the volume <span class="texhtml"><i>V</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>(<i>r</i>)</span> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-ball" title="N-ball"><i>n</i>-dimensional ball</a> of radius <i>r</i> in Euclidean <i>n</i>-dimensional space, and the surface area <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i><sub><i>n</i>−1</sub>(<i>r</i>)</span> of its boundary, the <a href="/wiki/N-sphere" title="N-sphere">(<i>n</i>−1)-dimensional sphere</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[176]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle V_{n}(r)={\frac {\pi ^{n/2}}{\Gamma \left({\frac {n}{2}}+1\right)}}r^{n},}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>n</mi>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_theory_and_Riemann_zeta_function">Number theory and Riemann zeta function</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pr%C3%BCfer.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="901" data-file-width="1255" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Pr%C3%BCfer.png/220px-Pr%C3%BCfer.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Pr%C3%BCfer.png/330px-Pr%C3%BCfer.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Pr%C3%BCfer.png/440px-Pr%C3%BCfer.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pr%C3%BCfer.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Each prime has an associated <a href="/wiki/Pr%C3%BCfer_group" title="Prüfer group">Prüfer group</a>, which are arithmetic localizations of the circle.  The <a href="/wiki/L-function" title="L-function">L-functions</a> of analytic number theory are also localized in each prime <i>p</i>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="181" data-file-width="401" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg/220px-ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg/330px-ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg/440px-ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Solution of the Basel problem using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weil_conjecture_on_Tamagawa_numbers" title="Weil conjecture on Tamagawa numbers">Weil conjecture</a>: the value of <span class="texhtml"><i>ζ</i>(2)</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9_half-plane_model" title="Poincaré half-plane model">hyperbolic</a> area of a fundamental domain of the <a href="/wiki/Modular_group" title="Modular group">modular group</a>, times <span class="texhtml"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>/2</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>ζ</i>(<i>s</i>)</span> is used in many areas of mathematics. When evaluated at <span class="texhtml"><i>s</i> = 2</span> it can be written as
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \zeta (2)={\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\cdots }" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ζ<!-- ζ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<msup>
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<msup>
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<msup>
<mn>3</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mo>⋯<!-- ⋯ --></mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \zeta (2)={\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\cdots }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \zeta (2)={\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\cdots }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/581714c279e80acf87edfb221004f575d4770e97" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:27.568ex; height:5.676ex;"/></div>
Finding a <a href="/wiki/Closed-form_expression" title="Closed-form expression">simple solution</a> for this infinite series was a famous problem in mathematics called the <a href="/wiki/Basel_problem" title="Basel problem">Basel problem</a>. <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> solved it in 1735 when he showed it was equal to <span class="texhtml">π<sup>2</sup>/6</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Posamentier_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Posamentier-90">[87]</a></sup> Euler's result leads to the <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a> result that the probability of two random numbers being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relatively_prime" title="Relatively prime">relatively prime</a> (that is, having no shared factors) is equal to <span class="texhtml">6/π<sup>2</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[180]</a></sup> This probability is based on the observation that the probability that any number is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divisible" title="Divisible">divisible</a> by a prime <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i></span> is <span class="texhtml">1/<i>p</i></span> (for example, every 7th integer is divisible by 7.) Hence the probability that two numbers are both divisible by this prime is <span class="texhtml">1/<i>p</i><sup>2</sup></span>, and the probability that at least one of them is not is <span class="texhtml">1 − 1/<i>p</i><sup>2</sup></span>. For distinct primes, these divisibility events are mutually independent; so the probability that two numbers are relatively prime is given by a product over all primes:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[181]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\prod _{p}^{\infty }\left(1-{\frac {1}{p^{2}}}\right)&amp;=\left(\prod _{p}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{1-p^{-2}}}\right)^{-1}\\[4pt]&amp;={\frac {1}{1+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\cdots }}\\[4pt]&amp;={\frac {1}{\zeta (2)}}={\frac {6}{\pi ^{2}}}\approx 61\%.\end{aligned}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true" rowspacing="0.7em 0.7em 0.3em">

<mo>=</mo>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\prod _{p}^{\infty }\left(1-{\frac {1}{p^{2}}}\right)&amp;=\left(\prod _{p}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{1-p^{-2}}}\right)^{-1}\\[4pt]&amp;={\frac {1}{1+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\cdots }}\\[4pt]&amp;={\frac {1}{\zeta (2)}}={\frac {6}{\pi ^{2}}}\approx 61\%.\end{aligned}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\prod _{p}^{\infty }\left(1-{\frac {1}{p^{2}}}\right)&amp;=\left(\prod _{p}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{1-p^{-2}}}\right)^{-1}\\[4pt]&amp;={\frac {1}{1+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\cdots }}\\[4pt]&amp;={\frac {1}{\zeta (2)}}={\frac {6}{\pi ^{2}}}\approx 61\%.\end{aligned}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/33ad11b6609d91487577949c7a42872afdc33a36" style="vertical-align: -11.171ex; width:36.922ex; height:23.509ex;"/></div>
</p><p>This probability can be used in conjunction with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Random_number_generator" title="Random number generator">random number generator</a> to approximate <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> using a Monte Carlo approach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>The solution to the Basel problem implies that the geometrically derived quantity <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is connected in a deep way to the distribution of prime numbers.  This is a special case of <a href="/wiki/Weil%27s_conjecture_on_Tamagawa_numbers" title="Weil's conjecture on Tamagawa numbers">Weil's conjecture on Tamagawa numbers</a>, which asserts the equality of similar such infinite products of <i>arithmetic</i> quantities, localized at each prime <i>p</i>, and a <i>geometrical</i> quantity: the reciprocal of the volume of a certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Locally_symmetric_space" title="Locally symmetric space">locally symmetric space</a>.  In the case of the Basel problem, it is the <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_3-manifold" title="Hyperbolic 3-manifold">hyperbolic 3-manifold</a> <span class="texhtml"><a href="/wiki/SL2(R)" title="SL2(R)">SL<sub>2</sub>(<b>R</b>)</a>/<a href="/wiki/Modular_group" title="Modular group">SL<sub>2</sub>(<b>Z</b>)</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>The zeta function also satisfies Riemann's functional equation, which involves <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> as well as the gamma function:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \zeta (s)=2^{s}\pi ^{s-1}\ \sin \left({\frac {\pi s}{2}}\right)\ \Gamma (1-s)\ \zeta (1-s).}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>Furthermore, the derivative of the zeta function satisfies
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \exp(-\zeta '(0))={\sqrt {2\pi }}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \exp(-\zeta '(0))={\sqrt {2\pi }}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \exp(-\zeta '(0))={\sqrt {2\pi }}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d2907a3d1a8927b4fd2a276f0463569cc1f77b6f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:20.12ex; height:3.176ex;"/></div>
A consequence is that <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> can be obtained from the <a href="/wiki/Functional_determinant" title="Functional determinant">functional determinant</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_oscillator" title="Harmonic oscillator">harmonic oscillator</a>.  This functional determinant can be computed via a product expansion, and is equivalent to the Wallis product formula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[184]</a></sup>  The calculation can be recast in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, specifically the <a href="/wiki/Calculus_of_variations" title="Calculus of variations">variational approach</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">spectrum of the hydrogen atom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourier_series">Fourier series</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg/220px-2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg/330px-2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg/440px-2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> appears in characters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-adic_numbers" title="P-adic numbers">p-adic numbers</a> (shown), which are elements of a <a href="/wiki/Pr%C3%BCfer_group" title="Prüfer group">Prüfer group</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tate%27s_thesis" title="Tate's thesis">Tate's thesis</a> makes heavy use of this machinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[186]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> also appears naturally in <a href="/wiki/Fourier_series" title="Fourier series">Fourier series</a> of <a href="/wiki/Periodic_function" title="Periodic function">periodic functions</a>.  Periodic functions are functions on the group <span class="texhtml"><b>T</b> =<b>R</b>/<b>Z</b></span> of fractional parts of real numbers.  The Fourier decomposition shows that a complex-valued function <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i></span> on <span class="texhtml"><b>T</b></span> can be written as an infinite linear superposition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unitary_character" title="Unitary character">unitary characters</a> of <span class="texhtml"><b>T</b></span>.   That is, continuous <a href="/wiki/Group_homomorphism" title="Group homomorphism">group homomorphisms</a> from <span class="texhtml"><b>T</b></span> to the <a href="/wiki/Circle_group" title="Circle group">circle group</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>U</i>(1)</span> of unit modulus complex numbers.  It is a theorem that every character of <span class="texhtml"><b>T</b></span> is one of the complex exponentials <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle e_{n}(x)=e^{2\pi inx}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>n</mi>
<mi>x</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle e_{n}(x)=e^{2\pi inx}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle e_{n}(x)=e^{2\pi inx}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/142bcbcd2610f5640cdfd50166a105624164d007" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.113ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>.
</p><p>There is a unique character on <span class="texhtml"><b>T</b></span>, up to complex conjugation, that is a group isomorphism.  Using the <a href="/wiki/Haar_measure" title="Haar measure">Haar measure</a> on the circle group, the constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is half the magnitude of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radon%E2%80%93Nikodym_derivative" title="Radon–Nikodym derivative">Radon–Nikodym derivative</a> of this character.  The other characters have derivatives whose magnitudes are positive integral multiples of 2<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicolas_Bourbaki_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nicolas_Bourbaki-21">[19]</a></sup>  As a result, the constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is the unique number such that the group <b>T</b>, equipped with its Haar measure, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontrjagin_dual" title="Pontrjagin dual">Pontrjagin dual</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Lattice_(group)" title="Lattice (group)">lattice</a> of integral multiples of 2<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDymMcKean1972Chapter_4_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDymMcKean1972Chapter_4-190">[187]</a></sup>  This is a version of the one-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Poisson_summation_formula" title="Poisson summation formula">Poisson summation formula</a>.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modular_forms_and_theta_functions">Modular forms and theta functions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lattice_with_tau.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="134" data-file-width="158" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ca/Lattice_with_tau.svg/220px-Lattice_with_tau.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ca/Lattice_with_tau.svg/330px-Lattice_with_tau.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ca/Lattice_with_tau.svg/440px-Lattice_with_tau.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lattice_with_tau.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Theta functions transform under the <a href="/wiki/Lattice_(group)" title="Lattice (group)">lattice</a> of periods of an elliptic curve.</div></div></div>
<p>The constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is connected in a deep way with the theory of <a href="/wiki/Modular_form" title="Modular form">modular forms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theta_function" title="Theta function">theta functions</a>.  For example, the <a href="/wiki/Chudnovsky_algorithm" title="Chudnovsky algorithm">Chudnovsky algorithm</a> involves in an essential way the <a href="/wiki/J-invariant" title="J-invariant">j-invariant</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Elliptic_curve" title="Elliptic curve">elliptic curve</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Modular_form" title="Modular form">Modular forms</a> are <a href="/wiki/Holomorphic_function" title="Holomorphic function">holomorphic functions</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_half_plane" title="Upper half plane">upper half plane</a> characterized by their transformation properties under the <a href="/wiki/Modular_group" title="Modular group">modular group</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {SL} _{2}(\mathbb {Z} )}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">L</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">Z</mi>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>

which is a kind of modular form called a <a href="/wiki/Jacobi_form" title="Jacobi form">Jacobi form</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mumford_1983_1–117_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mumford_1983_1–117-191">[188]</a></sup>  This is sometimes written in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Nome_(mathematics)" title="Nome (mathematics)">nome</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle q=e^{\pi i\tau }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>The constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is the unique constant making the Jacobi theta function an <a href="/wiki/Automorphic_form" title="Automorphic form">automorphic form</a>, which means that it transforms in a specific way. </math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \theta (z+\tau ,\tau )=e^{-\pi i\tau -2\pi iz}\theta (z,\tau ),}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/94df3eda20c1a72c699a40cf8b4e0ef49801f601" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:29.568ex; height:3.176ex;"/></div>
which implies that <span class="texhtml">θ</span> transforms as a representation under the discrete <a href="/wiki/Heisenberg_group" title="Heisenberg group">Heisenberg group</a>.  General modular forms and other <a href="/wiki/Theta_function" title="Theta function">theta functions</a> also involve <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, once again because of the <a href="/wiki/Stone%E2%80%93von_Neumann_theorem" title="Stone–von Neumann theorem">Stone–von Neumann theorem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mumford_1983_1–117_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mumford_1983_1–117-191">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cauchy_distribution_and_potential_theory">Cauchy distribution and potential theory</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Witch_of_Agnesi,_construction.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="223" data-file-width="398" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Witch_of_Agnesi%2C_construction.svg/220px-Witch_of_Agnesi%2C_construction.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Witch_of_Agnesi%2C_construction.svg/330px-Witch_of_Agnesi%2C_construction.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Witch_of_Agnesi%2C_construction.svg/440px-Witch_of_Agnesi%2C_construction.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Witch_of_Agnesi,_construction.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Witch_of_Agnesi" title="Witch of Agnesi">Witch of Agnesi</a>, named for <a href="/wiki/Maria_Gaetana_Agnesi" title="Maria Gaetana Agnesi">Maria Agnesi</a> (1718–1799), is a geometrical construction of the graph of the Cauchy distribution.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="606" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif/220px-2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif/330px-2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif/440px-2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cauchy distribution governs the passage of <a href="/wiki/Brownian_motion" title="Brownian motion">Brownian particles</a> through a membrane.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cauchy_distribution" title="Cauchy distribution">Cauchy distribution</a>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle g(x)={\frac {1}{\pi }}\cdot {\frac {1}{x^{2}+1}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

is a <a href="/wiki/Probability_density_function" title="Probability density function">probability density function</a>.  The total probability is equal to one, owing to the integral:

</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shannon_entropy" title="Shannon entropy">Shannon entropy</a> of the Cauchy distribution is equal to <span class="texhtml">ln(4π)</span>, which also involves <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.
</p><p>The Cauchy distribution plays an important role in <a href="/wiki/Potential_theory" title="Potential theory">potential theory</a> because it is the simplest <a href="/wiki/Furstenberg_boundary" title="Furstenberg boundary">Furstenberg measure</a>, the classical <a href="/wiki/Poisson_kernel" title="Poisson kernel">Poisson kernel</a> associated with a <a href="/wiki/Brownian_motion" title="Brownian motion">Brownian motion</a> in a half-plane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[189]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjugate_harmonic_function" title="Conjugate harmonic function">Conjugate harmonic functions</a> and so also the <a href="/wiki/Hilbert_transform" title="Hilbert transform">Hilbert transform</a> are associated with the asymptotics of the Poisson kernel. The Hilbert transform <i>H</i> is the integral transform given by the <a href="/wiki/Cauchy_principal_value" title="Cauchy principal value">Cauchy principal value</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Singular_integral" title="Singular integral">singular integral</a>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle Hf(t)={\frac {1}{\pi }}\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }{\frac {f(x)\,dx}{x-t}}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo>=</mo>

</mfrac>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Hf(t)={\frac {1}{\pi }}\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }{\frac {f(x)\,dx}{x-t}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle Hf(t)={\frac {1}{\pi }}\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }{\frac {f(x)\,dx}{x-t}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2cd815ba69c72a4cb8e65180491f6db68eda5a12" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:25.312ex; height:6.343ex;"/></div>
</p><p>The constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is the unique (positive) normalizing factor such that <i>H</i> defines a <a href="/wiki/Linear_complex_structure" title="Linear complex structure">linear complex structure</a> on the Hilbert space of square-integrable real-valued functions on the real line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[190]</a></sup>  The Hilbert transform, like the Fourier transform, can be characterized purely in terms of its transformation properties on the Hilbert space <span class="texhtml">L<sup>2</sup>(<b>R</b>)</span>: up to a normalization factor, it is the unique bounded linear operator that commutes with positive dilations and anti-commutes with all reflections of the real line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[191]</a></sup>  The constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is the unique normalizing factor that makes this transformation unitary.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_Mandelbrot_set">In the Mandelbrot set</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg"><img alt="An complex black shape on a blue background." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg/220px-Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg/330px-Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg/440px-Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a> can be used to approximate <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>An occurrence of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> in the <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">fractal</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a> was discovered by David Boll in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KA_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-KA-195">[192]</a></sup> He examined the behaviour of the Mandelbrot set near the "neck" at <span class="texhtml">(−0.75, 0)</span>. When the number of iterations until divergence for the point <span class="texhtml">(−0.75, <i>ε</i>)</span> is multiplied by <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">ε</span>, the result approaches <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> as <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">ε</span> approaches zero. The point <span class="texhtml">(0.25 + <i>ε</i>, 0)</span> at the cusp of the large "valley" on the right side of the Mandelbrot set behaves similarly: the number of iterations until divergence multiplied by the square root of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">ε</span> tends to <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KA_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-KA-195">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_mathematics">Outside mathematics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Describing_physical_phenomena">Describing physical phenomena</span></h3>
<p>Although not a <a href="/wiki/Physical_constant" title="Physical constant">physical constant</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> appears routinely in equations describing fundamental principles of the universe, often because of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>'s relationship to the circle and to <a href="/wiki/Spherical_coordinate_system" title="Spherical coordinate system">spherical coordinate systems</a>. A simple formula from the field of <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a> gives the approximate period <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i></span> of a simple <a href="/wiki/Pendulum" title="Pendulum">pendulum</a> of length <span class="texhtml"><i>L</i></span>, swinging with a small amplitude (<span class="texhtml"><i>g</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">earth's gravitational acceleration</a>):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[194]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle T\approx 2\pi {\sqrt {\frac {L}{g}}}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo>≈<!-- ≈ --></mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>

<mfrac>

<mi>g</mi>
</mfrac>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T\approx 2\pi {\sqrt {\frac {L}{g}}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle T\approx 2\pi {\sqrt {\frac {L}{g}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bd035c36f4530cfdbe4d650dad9a9fb96600c271" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:12.619ex; height:7.509ex;"/></div>
One of the key formulae of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heisenberg%27s_uncertainty_principle" title="Heisenberg's uncertainty principle">Heisenberg's uncertainty principle</a>, which shows that the uncertainty in the measurement of a particle's position (Δ<span class="texhtml"><i>x</i></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> (Δ<span class="texhtml"><i>p</i></span>) cannot both be arbitrarily small at the same time (where <span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck's constant">Planck's constant</a>):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[195]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta x\,\Delta p\geq {\frac {h}{4\pi }}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>The fact that <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> is approximately equal to 3 plays a role in the relatively long lifetime of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthopositronium" title="Orthopositronium">orthopositronium</a>. where <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i></span> is the mass of the electron.

</p><p>The field of <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a> contains <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> in <a href="/wiki/Stokes%27_law" title="Stokes' law">Stokes' law</a>, which approximates the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drag_force" title="Drag force">frictional force</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>F</i></span> exerted on small, <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">spherical</a> objects of radius <span class="texhtml"><i>R</i></span>, moving with velocity <span class="texhtml"><i>v</i></span> in a <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluid</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynamic_viscosity" title="Dynamic viscosity">dynamic viscosity</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>η</i></span>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[198]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F=6\pi \eta Rv.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle F=6\pi \eta Rv.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e327b1b8e506d7dd945915675ce1ad287e53cdc" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:12.042ex; height:2.676ex;"/></div>
</p><p>In electromagnetics, the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permeability" title="Vacuum permeability">vacuum permeability</a> constant <i>μ</i><sub>0</sub> appears in <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">Maxwell's equations</a>, which describe the properties of <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic</a> fields and <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>. Before 20 May 2019, it was defined as exactly

</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \mu _{0}=4\pi \times 10^{-7}{\text{ H/m}}\approx 1.2566370614\ldots \times 10^{-6}{\text{ N/A}}^{2}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e8fa061c51aebf2f41aee0bbc787b6f599fd4019" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:55.04ex; height:3.343ex;"/></div>
A relation for the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> in vacuum, <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span> can be derived from Maxwell's equations in the medium of <a href="/wiki/Vacuum#In_electromagnetism" title="Vacuum">classical vacuum</a> using a relationship between <i>μ</i><sub>0</sub> and the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permittivity" title="Vacuum permittivity">electric constant (vacuum permittivity)</a>, <span class="texhtml"><i>ε</i><sub>0</sub></span> in SI units:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle c={1 \over {\sqrt {\mu _{0}\varepsilon _{0}}}}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
