<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle c={1 \over {\sqrt {\mu _{0}\varepsilon _{0}}}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle c={1 \over {\sqrt {\mu _{0}\varepsilon _{0}}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0f03fbd13a7575de6344420c90dbd26a1c6f9784" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:12.118ex; height:6.176ex;"/></div>
</p><p>Under ideal conditions (uniform gentle slope on a homogeneously erodible substrate), the <a href="/wiki/Sinuosity" title="Sinuosity">sinuosity</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Meander" title="Meander">meandering</a> river approaches <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. The sinuosity is the ratio between the actual length and the straight-line distance from source to mouth. Faster currents along the outside edges of a river's bends cause more erosion than along the inside edges, thus pushing the bends even farther out, and increasing the overall loopiness of the river. However, that loopiness eventually causes the river to double back on itself in places and "short-circuit", creating an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ox-bow_lake" title="Ox-bow lake">ox-bow lake</a> in the process. The balance between these two opposing factors leads to an average ratio of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> between the actual length and the direct distance between source and mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorizing_digits">Memorizing digits</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Piphilology" title="Piphilology">Piphilology</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Piphilology" title="Piphilology">Piphilology</a> is the practice of memorizing large numbers of digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A445_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-A445-204">[201]</a></sup> and world-records are kept by the <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a></i>.  The record for memorizing digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, certified by Guinness World Records, is 70,000 digits, recited in India by Rajveer Meena in 9 hours and 27 minutes on 21 March 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[202]</a></sup>  In 2006, <a href="/wiki/Akira_Haraguchi" title="Akira Haraguchi">Akira Haraguchi</a>, a retired Japanese engineer, claimed to have recited 100,000 decimal places, but the claim was not verified by Guinness World Records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-japantimes_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-japantimes-206">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>One common technique is to memorize a story or poem in which the word lengths represent the digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>: The first word has three letters, the second word has one, the third has four, the fourth has one, the fifth has five, and so on. Such memorization aids are called <a href="/wiki/Mnemonic" title="Mnemonic">mnemonics</a>. An early example of a mnemonic for pi, originally devised by English scientist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Hopwood_Jeans" title="James Hopwood Jeans">James Jeans</a>, is "How I want a drink, alcoholic of course, after the heavy lectures involving quantum mechanics."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A445_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-A445-204">[201]</a></sup> When a poem is used, it is sometimes referred to as a <i>piem</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[204]</a></sup> Poems for memorizing <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> have been composed in several languages in addition to English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A445_204-2"><a href="#cite_note-A445-204">[201]</a></sup>  Record-setting <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> memorizers typically do not rely on poems, but instead use methods such as remembering number patterns and the <a href="/wiki/Method_of_loci" title="Method of loci">method of loci</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>A few authors have used the digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> to establish a new form of <a href="/wiki/Constrained_writing" title="Constrained writing">constrained writing</a>, where the word lengths are required to represent the digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Cadaeic_Cadenza" title="Cadaeic Cadenza">Cadaeic Cadenza</a></i> contains the first 3835 digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> in this manner,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[206]</a></sup> and the full-length book <i>Not a Wake</i> contains 10,000 words, each representing one digit of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KeithNAW_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-KeithNAW-210">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pi_pie2.jpg"><img alt="Pi Pie at Delft University" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="577" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Pi_pie2.jpg/220px-Pi_pie2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Pi_pie2.jpg/330px-Pi_pie2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Pi_pie2.jpg/440px-Pi_pie2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pi_pie2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pi pie. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pies" title="Pies">Pies</a> are circular, and "pie" and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homophones" title="Homophones">homophones</a>, making pie a frequent subject of pi <a href="/wiki/Pun" title="Pun">puns</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Perhaps because of the simplicity of its definition and its ubiquitous presence in formulae, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> has been represented in popular culture more than other mathematical constructs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2008 <a href="/wiki/Open_University" title="Open University">Open University</a> and <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> documentary co-production, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_Maths" title="The Story of Maths">The Story of Maths</a></i>, aired in October 2008 on <a href="/wiki/BBC_Four" title="BBC Four">BBC Four</a>, British mathematician <a href="/wiki/Marcus_du_Sautoy" title="Marcus du Sautoy">Marcus du Sautoy</a> shows a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Information_graphics" title="Information graphics">visualization</a> of the – historically first exact – <a href="/wiki/Madhava_of_Sangamagrama#The_value_of_π_(pi)" title="Madhava of Sangamagrama">formula for calculating <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></a> when visiting India and exploring its contributions to trigonometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_2008_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2008-212">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Palais_de_la_D%C3%A9couverte" title="Palais de la Découverte">Palais de la Découverte</a> (a science museum in Paris) there is a circular room known as the <i>pi room</i>. On its wall are inscribed 707 digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. The digits are large wooden characters attached to the dome-like ceiling. The digits were based on an 1873 calculation by English mathematician <a href="/wiki/William_Shanks" title="William Shanks">William Shanks</a>, which included an error beginning at the 528th digit. The error was detected in 1946 and corrected in 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a>'s 1985 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Contact_(novel)" title="Contact (novel)">Contact</a></i> it is suggested that the creator of the universe buried a message deep within the digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[211]</a></sup> The digits of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> have also been incorporated into the lyrics of the song "Pi" from the 2005 album <i><a href="/wiki/Aerial_(album)" title="Aerial (album)">Aerial</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Kate_Bush" title="Kate Bush">Kate Bush</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[212]</a></sup> In the 1967 <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series" title="Star Trek: The Original Series">Star Trek</a></i> episode "<a href="/wiki/Wolf_in_the_Fold" title="Wolf in the Fold">Wolf in the Fold</a>", an out-of-control computer is contained by being instructed to "Compute to the last digit the value of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-life-of-pi_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-life-of-pi-49">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, <a href="/wiki/Pi_Day" title="Pi Day">Pi Day</a> falls on 14 March (written 3/14 in the US style), and is popular among students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-life-of-pi_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-life-of-pi-49">[46]</a></sup> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> and its digital representation are often used by self-described "math <a href="/wiki/Geek" title="Geek">geeks</a>" for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inside_joke" title="Inside joke">inside jokes</a> among mathematically and technologically minded groups. A <a href="/wiki/Cheering#Chants_in_North_American_sports" title="Cheering">college cheer</a> variously attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Rensselaer_Polytechnic_Institute" title="Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute">Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute</a> includes "3.14159".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[214]</a></sup> Pi Day in 2015 was particularly significant because the date and time 3/14/15 9:26:53 reflected many more digits of pi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[216]</a></sup> In parts of the world where dates are commonly noted in day/month/year format, 22 July represents "Pi Approximation Day," as 22/7 = 3.142857.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 2011 auction for <a href="/wiki/Nortel" title="Nortel">Nortel</a>'s portfolio of valuable technology patents, <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> made a series of unusually specific bids based on mathematical and scientific constants, including <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FP_2011_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FP_2011-221">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="tau"></span> 
In 1958 <a href="/wiki/Albert_Eagle" title="Albert Eagle">Albert Eagle</a> <a href="/wiki/Turn_(angle)#Proposals_for_a_single_letter_to_represent_2π" title="Turn (angle)">proposed</a> replacing <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> by <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">τ</span> (<a href="/wiki/Tau" title="Tau">tau</a>), where <span class="texhtml"><i>τ</i> = <i>π</i>/2</span>, to simplify formulas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AE1958_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-AE1958-222">[219]</a></sup> However, no other authors are known to use <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">τ</span> in this way. Some people use a different value, <span class="texhtml"><i>τ</i> = 2<i>π</i> = 6.28318...</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[220]</a></sup> arguing that <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">τ</span>, as the number of radians in one <a href="/wiki/Turn_(angle)" title="Turn (angle)">turn</a>, or as the ratio of a circle's circumference to its radius rather than its diameter, is more natural than <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> and simplifies many formulas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[222]</a></sup> However, this use of <span class="texhtml">τ</span> has not made its way into mainstream mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[223]</a></sup> Tau was added to the <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python programming language</a> (as math.tau) in version 3.6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1897, an amateur mathematician attempted to persuade the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_General_Assembly" title="Indiana General Assembly">Indiana legislature</a> to pass the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Pi_Bill" title="Indiana Pi Bill">Indiana Pi Bill</a>, which described a method to <a href="/wiki/Squaring_the_circle" title="Squaring the circle">square the circle</a> and contained text that implied various incorrect values for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, including 3.2. The bill is notorious as an attempt to establish a value of mathematical constant by legislative fiat. The bill was passed by the Indiana House of Representatives, but rejected by the Senate, meaning it did not become a law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[225]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_computer_culture">In computer culture</span></h3>
<p>In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Internet_culture" title="Internet culture">internet culture</a>, individuals and organizations frequently pay homage to the number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Computer_scientist" title="Computer scientist">computer scientist</a> <a href="/wiki/Donald_Knuth" title="Donald Knuth">Donald Knuth</a> let the version numbers of his program <a href="/wiki/TeX" title="TeX">TeX</a> approach <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>. The versions are 3, 3.1, 3.14, and so forth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[226]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-183"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-183">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This theorem was proved by <a href="/wiki/Ernesto_Ces%C3%A0ro" title="Ernesto Cesàro">Ernesto Cesàro</a> in 1881. For a more rigorous proof than the intuitive and informal one given here, see <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHardy2008">Hardy, G. H. (2008). <i>An Introduction to the Theory of Numbers</i>. Oxford University Press.
